The draft guidelines will be adopted by the Commission this autumn.

The draft guidelines will be presented to the European Parliament and will be discussed with the Member States in the appropriate Management Committee. The opinions of all parties concerned will, of course, be taken into account.

The Commission expects that Greece will play a full role in the relevant programmes concerning cross-border, transnational and interregional co-operation, including increasing co-operation with the countries of Central and Eastern Europe.

 

Question no 47 by Efstratios Korakas (H-0435/99)
 Subject: Conservation of the temple of Agrotera Artemis in Athens
 

The site of the temple of Agrotera Artemis in Athens - part of a retaining wall of which survives - is an extremely important feature of the capital's archaeological remains and an integral part of the cultural heritage. Owing to the Greek Government's indifference and deliberate procrastination in taking over the site and incorporating it into the integrated programme for the archaeological sites of Athens, there is now a risk that the site will be transformed into a private area for commercial enterprise.

What immediate action and initiatives will the Commission take to prevent any degradation of the archaeological and cultural value of the site and will it use the funds provided under the programmes for the protection and preservation of the European cultural heritage to take over the site, develop it appropriately and integrate it into the larger archaeological site of the Olympieion?

 
  
 

According to Article 151 of the EC Treaty on the European Union, the protection and conservation of cultural heritage is, exclusively, the competence of the national competent authorities.

To the knowledge of the Commission, the national competent authorities (Ministry of Culture) have taken all the necessary immediate measures for the protection of the site where the very few remains of the temple of Agrotera Artemis are situated in view of its inclusion into the integrated programme for the archaeological sites of Athens. Actually, the legal process for the expropriation of the private old buildings on the site is well in progress and once completed the site will be cleared of these buildings so that it can be developed so as to enhance the value of the historic site of the river Ilissos as well as the memory of the few remnants of the temple of Agrotera Artemis.

The funding of expropriation of archaeological sites is not foreseen under the programmes for the protection and preservation of European cultural heritage; the Programme ‘Culture 2000’, when adopted, will provide funding possibilities for the enhancement of cultural co-operation at a European level.

 

Question no 48 by John Purvis (H-0439/99)
 Subject: Special assistance to Scotland
 

In order to satisfy Member States’ environmental commitments, higher-than-inflation increases have been put on fuel taxes.

Remote rural areas such as the Highlands and Islands of Scotland are suffering directly from these excessive burdens. This has severe repercussions for the region. Special assistance of either a financial or infrastructural nature is required to take account of the specific nature of these areas.

What measures does the Commission propose to take to help the citizens in the Highlands and Islands meet these additional costs?

 
  
 

The Commission has no jurisdiction to deal with the question asked, which is a matter solely for the national authorities concerned.

 

Question no 49 by Camilo Nogueira Román (H-0442/99)
 Subject: Unemployment rate in Galicia
 

In Galicia, 16% of the entire population are unemployed and the number of people working in agriculture is plummeting. Does the Commission think that a pilot scheme for job creation in Galicia during the 2000-2006 period could be drawn up and implemented pursuant to the Amsterdam Treaty?

 
  
 

The Treaty of Amsterdam mandates that the Commission and the Member States work towards developing a co-ordinated strategy for employment and particularly for promoting a skilled, trained and adaptable workforce and labour markets responsive to economic changes. This strategy encompasses the co-ordination of Member States’ employment policies on the basis of commonly agreed employment guidelines, the continuation and development of a co-ordinated macroeconomic policy and an efficient internal market. The employment package, approved by the Commission on 8 September, sets the coherent framework and the specific set of lines of action for national employment policies, for which the new ESF will provide budgetary support at Community level.

In this context, the new Structural Funds regulations approved for the new programming period 2000-2006 state that, in order to reinforce the integrated approach to development, steps should be taken – with due regard to the specific features of the regions concerned – to ensure consistency between the operations of the various Funds and the Community policies, the strategy for employment, the economic and social policies of the Member States and the regional policies of the Member States.

The European Social Fund will be important in underpinning the European Employment Strategy. A new Community Support Framework will be approved early next year based on the plans that will be introduced by Member States in the coming months. During the negotiations with the Member States, the Commission will encourage any action that effectively addresses an identified national or regional problem and that is consistent with the National Action Plan for employment, the regulation of the European Social Fund, and the evaluation exercises carried out by the Member States.

 

Question no 50 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H-0444/99)
 Subject: Construction of a nuclear power station in southern Morocco
 

In the light of press reports concerning the construction of a nuclear power station for sea water desalination purposes in southern Morocco (and in close proximity to the Canary Islands), is the Commission considering the possibility of granting aid to Morocco to enable some other type of energy – cleaner and less hazardous – to be used?

 
  
 

The Commission has received no direct information concerning the plan to construct a nuclear power station.

On the basis of an analysis of the items which have appeared in the press, we have established that the project in question concerns the construction of a nuclear power station for the desalination of seawater in the coastal area of the South of Morocco, in the region of Tan-Tan. This project, dating from August 1998, envisaged that the 10 MW reactor would be a model similar to that used in the countries of the former USSR (NHR-5) and would have to be installed as a result of a co-operation agreement between Morocco (Ministry of Foreign Affairs, National Electricity Office, National Drinking Water Office, CNESTEN) and China (Ministry of Science and Technology, ITEN and the University of Tsinghua).

It would appear that the agreement regarding this project was signed in December 1998.

 

Question no 51 by John Joseph McCartin (H-0445/99)
 Subject: Reg. 2377/90­Max. Residue Limits of Veterinary medicinal products in foodstuffs of animal origin
 

Is the Commission aware of the difficulties being created by the introduction of Council Regulation 2377/90(1) laying down a Community procedure for the establishment of maximum residue limits of medicinal products in foodstuffs and does the Commission have any proposal to exempt horses which are kept for sport and as pets and which will never be part of the food chain.

 
 
 

(1) OJ L 224 of 18.8.1990, p. 1

 

Question no 53 by Avril Doyle (H-0449/99)
 Subject: The present formulation of EU Council Regulation 2377/90 preventing the use of equine medicines for which no MRLs have been set prior to 1.1.2000
 

In view of the fact that consumers are fully entitled to guarantees that human health is not compromised by the presence in the food chain of any potentially unsafe residues; the economic importance in many Member States of the thoroughbred and sport horse industries; the concerns of veterinarians, breeders, welfare organisations, equestrian federations, Agriculture Ministers and concerned citizens; the fact that unlike other species whose primary purpose is food production, observance of a lengthy withdrawal period is viable where horses are concerned; that the Commission is well aware of the disastrous effects Regulation 2377/90(1) in its present form will have on the welfare and therapeutic treatment of horses from 1.1.2000 and subsequent economic impact thereof; that no formal Commission proposal has been brought forward despite meetings on this issue in 1998 and in 1999 during the German Presidency; when and in what form will the Commission honour its commitment (as voiced by Franz Fischler at the June Council) to address this urgent issue and ensure that a specific solution, from the range which has been proposed, is found guaranteeing the continued availability of all necessary equine medicines post 1.1.2000?

 
  
 

The Commission is aware of the problems raised by the honourable Members. As already announced at the June 1999 Agricultural Council, the Commission intends to propose an amendment of Regulation (EEC) No. 2377/90 and of Directive 81/851/EEC shortly.

One of the aims of these amendments will be to create a procedure whereby certain clearly distinguishable groups of food producing animals (e.g. sport horses) may not be regarded as food producing animals for the purposes of Regulation (EEC) No. 2377/90 and of Directive 81/851/EEC. This exception will allow the administration of a wide range of medicinal products (including substances for which a Maximum Residue Evaluation has not taken place) to the specific group(s) of animals (e.g. sport horses) covered by the exception. According to the preliminary draft which is currently being elaborated at the Commission, this exception would – of course – only be applicable if sufficient identification and control measures provided for by Community law allow such differentiation and provide a safeguard that meat of the animals concerned will never enter the human food chain.

The Commission intends to submit the above proposals to the European Parliament and Council under Article 152 paragraph 4 of the EC Treaty in early autumn 1999.

 
 

(1) OJ L 224 of 18.8.1990, p. 1.

 

Question no 52 by Konstantinos Alyssandrakis (H-0448/99)
 Subject: Measures to prevent the genocide of Serbs in Kosovo
 

The spokesman for the Office of the United Nations High Commission for Refugees (UNHCR), Mr Chris Jankowski, has warned that we are approaching the point where there will be a Serb-free Kosovo, an extremely deplorable situation where the Serbs are fleeing in droves and it is estimated that only 30,000 of the 250,000 Serbs living in Kosovo when the bombing stopped still remain in the region.

Reliable sources have reported massacres of Serbs, hundreds of cases of torture, kidnapping and extortion, the burning of 40,000 Serb homes and the existence of mass graves of Serbs killed after the arrival of KFOR, while it is estimated that 195,000 Serbs have fled Kosovo for Serbia and Montenegro.

What steps will the Commission take to prevent the genocide of Serbs in the region, curb the actions of the KLA, and ensure that a further round of ethnic cleansing does not take place before the very eyes of KFOR if not with their participation?

 
  
 

The Commission shares the concern of the Honourable Member concerning the situation of all ethnic minorities in Kosovo.

The Commission is participating fully in the work of the United Nations Mission in Kosovo (UNMIK) which, pursuant to UN Security Council Resolution 1244, is responsible for civil implementation in Kosovo. One of the tasks of UNMIK is to build a multiethnic society in Kosovo.

On the military side, KFOR is responsible for the implementation of the various military-technical agreements and undertakings, as well as general law and order until the establishment of a civilian police force. Various Member States have contributed personnel to KFOR.

Both the civil and military authorities are working to ensure a peaceful and multi-ethnic Kosovo in which all ethnic communities can co-exist.

 

Question no 54 by Ioannis Theonas (H-0450/99)
 Subject: Threat to public and consumer health caused by the irresponsible action of the agri-foodstuffs industry
 

More and more cases have been reported of foodstuffs dangerous to human health being placed on the market in the European Union. The ‘mad cow’ scare was followed by the dioxin crisis, and no one knows what will happen next. The problem is highlighted by the growing number of crises faced by the people of the EU, who at best are warned of the risk only after they have already consumed large quantities of the dangerous foodstuffs concerned. The food industry bears an enormous responsibility for this state of affairs, as a result of defective controls, technological methods and the use of raw materials authorised by the Member States without proper health controls.

What measures will the Commission take and what proposals will it submit to the Council for the protection of consumers and public health, in order to extend, increase and improve the effectiveness of controls on the food industry and, more generally, on agricultural production processes, so as to protect the public from the irresponsible behaviour which occurs when the search for profits is placed above the interests of public health?

 
  
 

The Commission legislates in the field of the control of foodstuffs in agreement with Member States, the implementation of these controls being their responsibility.

However, in the context of the revision of Community legislation concerning foodstuff hygiene, the Commission is preparing a draft regulation which should strengthen the protection of public health as well as food safety. This draft, based on the revision and harmonisation of existing texts, takes into account the following principles in particular:

. hygiene should be guaranteed throughout the food chain, from the stable to the table; the agricultural sector should, in particular, draw up hygiene codes applicable to primary production;

. operators must establish a strengthened system of controls in accordance with the principles of the HACCP (Hazard Analysis of Critical Control Points) system;

. the competent authorities must guarantee the public control of establishments and the products originating from those establishments;

. the traceability of foodstuffs and their ingredients must be guaranteed.

Furthermore, within the framework of the transfer of certain services from the Directorate-General responsible for agriculture to the Directorate responsible for consumer health and protection (SANCO), an in-depth study will be carried out regarding the most efficient measures to be implemented to guarantee the protection of public and consumer health. Other specific measures will then be proposed.

 

Question no 55 by Rosa Miguélez Ramos (H-0456/99)
 Subject: Galicia and the high-speed train
 

Mrs Loyola de Palacio, the candidate proposed by Mr Prodi for the post of Commissioner responsible for transport, stated whilst on holiday in Galicia that the high-speed rail network would extend as far as Galicia, even though neither the Spanish Government nor RENFE (Spanish national rail company) has any such plans; indeed, Galicia is, besides the Canary and Balearic islands, the only one of Spain’s autonomous communities which, according to RENFE’s plans, is not to be served by a high-speed train. Mrs Loyola de Palacio’s statement has caused confusion in Galicia since nobody knows whether she was speaking in her capacity as a former minister, as the probable future Commissioner responsible for transport (thereby compromising the Commission) or simply as a holidaymaker in Galicia.

Does Mrs Loyola de Palacio’s statement compromise the Commission? Is the Commission planning to build a high-speed rail link between Galicia and Madrid via Valladolid in the reasonably near future? Has the Spanish Government notified the Commission of any plan or project to link Galicia with central Spain by means of a high-speed train? Has the Galician Regional Government made any similar announcement?

 
  
 

The decision to build a high-speed rail link between Galicia and Madrid via Valladolid, as well as the corresponding planning, fall under the primary responsibility of Spain, in accordance with the subsidiarity principle.

The development of the Trans-European Transport Network is based on the decision of the European Parliament and the Council of 23 July 1996(1) on the Community guidelines for the Trans-European Network.

The maps annexed to this decision show the layout of the high-speed network planned for the year 2010.

In the specific case of Galicia, no concrete high-speed project toward the centre of Spain was envisaged at the time of the development of these guidelines. However, the maps of 1996 already point out a project for a high-speed link from Madrid to Valladolid, as well as an infrastructure upgrading project with a view to reaching a speed of about 200 km per hour from Valladolid towards Monforte of Lemos (Orense), followed by a conventional line towards Orense and Santiago. Furthermore, part of the 1997 Commission proposal to modify the 1996 Decision was to amend the title of the specific project No 8 from “Motorway Lisbon Valladolid” to “Multimodal link between Portugal-Spain and the rest of Europe”. The Commission accepted the amendment proposed by the European Parliament after the first reading to redefine the objectives of the specific project No 8 such that the connection of Galicia to the TEN is more visible.

Concerning the Spanish proposals to the Commission in the field of high-speed rail under the TEN guidelines of 1996, the current investment priority is the Madrid-Barcelona-French border line.

However, a process of revision of the TEN guidelines is foreseen in accordance with Article 21 of the decision of 1996. Within this framework, the European Parliament and the Member States will be fully involved in the revision process which should start as soon as possible.

 
 

(1) No 1692/96/EC (OJ L 2828, 9.9.1996)

 

Question no 56 by Carlos Candal (H-0457/99)
 Subject: EU-Morocco Fisheries Agreement
 

What is the current state of play with regard to the impending expiry of the EU-Morocco Fisheries Agreement?

What measures have been and will be taken by the Commission to ensure that the Community fleet can continue to fish in Moroccan waters?

 
 

Question no 58 by Fernando Pérez Royo (H-0459/99)
 Subject: Preliminary contacts with Morocco regarding fisheries
 

Will the Commission say whether it has engaged in preliminary contacts with the Moroccan authorities, bearing in mind that the existing EU-Morocco Fisheries Agreement will expire shortly?

 
  
 

The Commission has been authorised by the Council of 10 June 1999 to make preliminary contacts with the Moroccan Authorities with a view to jointly undertaking research into and development of new co-operation mechanisms in the fishing industry which will be of benefit to both parties.

Following the conclusions of the Council, an exchange of letters has taken place during June and July between the Commission and the Moroccan Authorities who have expressed their willingness to schedule a meeting to begin discussions between the two parties after 15 September 1999.

Contacts are currently under way between the Commission and the Moroccan administration to prepare for the start of these preliminary discussions.

 

Question no 57 by Pedro Aparicio Sánchez (H-0458/99)
 Subject: Financial compensation in connection with the expiry of the fisheries agreement with Morocco
 

The Community fishing fleet is concerned at the forthcoming expiry in November of the current EU-Morocco fisheries agreement.

What action is the Commission planning to take if the current agreement expires without a new one having been ratified?

Will financial compensation be made available by the EU to the Community fleet affected by any gap between the expiry of the current agreement and the entry into force of its successor?

What message can the Commission offer to the thousands of fisheries workers in the Canary Islands, Galicia, Andalucia and the Algarve, whose jobs are directly dependent on the EU-Morocco fisheries agreement and who are fearful of losing them in November, when that agreement expires?

 
  
 

The Commission would like to assure the honourable Member of the fact that it has implemented all the measures necessary to allow it to engage in new talks with Morocco. In particular, procedures allowing the Commission to have a new Community mandate for negotiations have been put in place.

With regard to possible financial compensation relating to a waiting period for fishermen, the honourable Member should note that, in the past and in a similar context, the Spanish Authorities have agreed to provide aid for fishermen and ship owners who have had to halt their fishing activities following the suspension of the fishing agreement with Morocco. As for the future, the Council is in the process of examining a proposed regulation which includes the provision of an aid system which could satisfy the concerns of the honourable Member.

The Commission is clearly extremely concerned about the possible economic and social implications in the event that a situation or solution satisfactory to the fishermen and the Kingdom of Morocco could not be found.

In any event, the Commission will do everything in its power to safeguard the interests of Community fishing in that part of the world.

 

Questions to the Council
Question no 20 by Konstantinos Alyssandrakis (H-0447/99)
 Subject: UN embargo against Iraq and doubling of infant mortality rate
 

In its latest report UNICEF has condemned the cruel effects of the sanctions against Iraq. The head of the organisation, Mrs Carol Bellamy, quotes the figures produced by the first full survey of the effects of the embargo over the nine years it has been in force. The figures show that the mortality rate for children under the age of five which, between 1984 and 1989, was around 56 per thousand births, has doubled since 1994 and reached the level of 131 per thousand. More than 500,000 children have died as a result of the inhuman cruelty of this long embargo.

What measures will the Council take following the publication of the UNICEF report, which once again shows that it is not Saddam Hussein who is suffering the effects of the embargo but the most vulnerable sections of the Iraqi population, particularly children, while the regime itself has stood firm?

 
  
 

The European Union is deeply concerned about the situation for the people of Iraq, in particular the children. According to the UNICEF survey, published in August this year, the mortality rate for children in southern and central Iraq has more than doubled since 1991, whereas the situation in this respect is better in the areas in northern Iraq not under the direct control of Baghdad.

The European Union considers that lasting security and stability in the region, as well as the living conditions of the Iraqi people, are the prime considerations that must guide the UN Security Council in its deliberations to reach agreement on arrangements, including sanctions, to ensure that Iraq does not – again – develop WMD capability. We hope that unity in the UN Security Council can be restored, in order to be able to alleviate the sufferings of the Iraqi people. However, the European Union is also aware that, in order to reach a solution on the issue of sanctions, increased co-operation from the Iraqi leadership will be necessary.

The European Union has been giving humanitarian aid to Iraq since 1991, to begin with mainly to the northern parts but later concentrated on the southern and central parts of Iraq. Following the “oil-for-food programme” of the UN, such aid has tried to complement that programme.

 

Question no 21 by Rosa Miguélez Ramos (H-0451/99)
 Subject: Concern over the expiry of the EU-Morocco Fisheries Agreement
 

Is the Council aware of the serious damage awaiting the Community fleet from November, when the existing fisheries agreement with Morocco expires?

What message can it send to the thousands of workers in the fisheries sector in Galicia, Andalucia, the Algarve and the Canary Islands, whose jobs depend directly on this agreement?

What measures does it intend to take to alleviate the losses which the Community fisheries fleet fishing in Moroccan fishing grounds will incur ?

 
 

Question no 22 by Carlos Candal (H-0452/99)
 Subject: Commission mandate for a new agreement with Morocco
 

What information can the Council provide on the mandate it is supposed to give to the Commission to enable the latter to enter into negotiations with the Kingdom of Morocco on a new fisheries agreement?

 
 

Question no 23 by Pedro Aparicio Sánchez (H-0453/99)
 Subject: Alternatives for the Community fleet fishing in Morocco
 

What alternatives can the Council offer to the Community fleet, which will be obliged to suspend its activities as of November, when the EU-Morocco Fisheries Agreement expires?

 
 

Question no 24 by Fernando Pérez Royo (H-0454/99)
 Subject: Future of the fleet fishing in Morocco
 

It is surprising that, with the fisheries agreement with Morocco due to expire in two months’ time, the Council has still not given the Commission a mandate to negotiate a new agreement or to extend the existing one.

What steps does the Council intend to take, given that the aforementioned agreement expires in November?

 
  
 

The Council is fully aware of the consequences that the expiry of the current fisheries agreement with Morocco at the end of November 1999 may have on the Community fleet fishing in Moroccan waters. Indeed, this question has been the subject of in-depth discussion for some time now at all levels in the Council and was discussed at the last Fisheries Council on 10 June 1999.

On that occasion, the Council reiterated the importance of bilateral fisheries relations with Morocco in the context of our global political and economic relations with that country. It also confirmed the socio-economic dimension for certain fisheries-dependent regions of the Community of a continuing substantive fisheries relationship with Morocco beyond the expiry of the current agreement. Against this background it urged the Commission to undertake as soon and as far as possible with Morocco the joint identification and elaboration of a new, mutually beneficial co-operative mechanism in the fisheries sector.

Finally, the Commission was to report to the Council on the outcome of these talks and to make any appropriate proposals.

The Council understands that the Commission has been exploring, internally, on an on-going basis, possible wider avenues of co-operation beyond the present agreement with Morocco with a view to presenting a recommendation for a negotiating mandate to Council; it has also been making efforts to set up talks with Morocco. However, the recent death of the King of Morocco has affected the administration in Morocco and a meeting planned for mid September 1999 has been deferred.

The Council, of course, will act with all due speed when the Commission comes forward with a Recommendation to negotiate any future arrangements between the European Community and Morocco in the fisheries sector.

In accordance with its conclusions, the Council will also consider any other proposals which the Commission may deem appropriate in this context.

 

Question no 25 by Esko Olavi Seppänen (H-0455/99)
 Subject: Ex-Commissioner Bangemann's pension
 

Commissioner Bangemann has announced that he is moving to work for a Spanish company before the term of office of the Commission has even finished. What EU-approved document authorises a Commissioner to be paid a special temporary allowance for 3 years even if he or she accepts a post and a salary from another activity, and what does the Council intend to do to put an end to this iniquitous practice, both in general and in Commissioner Bangemann’s specific case?

 
  
 

Article 7 of Regulation 422/67/EEC, 5/67 Euratom of the Council of 25 July 1967 determining the emoluments of the President and members of the Commission and of the President, Judges, Advocates-General and Registrar of the Court of Justice(1) provides that for three years after having ceased to hold office, a former member of the Commission shall receive a monthly transitional allowance of 40 to 65% (according to the duration of his service) of the basic salary which he was receiving when he ceased to hold office. Entitlement to this allowance shall cease if the former member of the Commission is reappointed to office in one of the institutions of the Communities.

If, during this three-year period, the person concerned takes up any new gainful activity, the amount by which his gross monthly remuneration (i.e. before deduction of taxes) together with the transitional allowance exceeds the remuneration (before deduction of taxes) which he was receiving as an active member of the Commission shall be deducted from the allowance. In calculating the amount of remuneration received for the new activity, all forms of remuneration shall be included, except those representing reimbursement of expenses.

Pursuant to Article 13 of the above-mentioned regulation, the transitional allowance provided for in Article 7 and any pension to which the person concerned is entitled by virtue of Articles 8 and 10 of this regulation shall not be drawn concurrently by the same person. In such a case, only that provision which is the most favourable to the claimant shall be applied.

For the time being, the Council is not considering amending these provisions.

As regards Mr Bangemann, it is recalled that on 9 July 1999 the Council decided to refer this case to the Court of Justice of the European Communities pursuant to the last sentence of the third subparagraph of Article 213(2) of the Treaty establishing the European Community and the corresponding provisions of the ECSC and EAEC Treaties(2). Pursuant to this article, the members of the Commission are particularly obliged to behave with integrity and discretion as regards the acceptance, after they have ceased to hold office, of certain appointments of benefits. In the event of any breach of these obligations, the Court of Justice may, on application by the Council or the Commission, rule that the Member concerned be deprived of his right to a pension or other benefits in its stead.

The Council’s Application in that sense was entered in the Register of the Court of Justice on 3 August 1999 as case C-290/99.

 
 

(1) OJ L 187 of 8 August 1967, p. 1
(2) Council Decision 1999/494/EC, ECSC, Euratom of 9 July 1999 on the referral of the case of Mr Bangemann to the Court of Justice, OJ L 192 of 24 July 1999, p. 55

 

Question no 26 by Richard Howitt (H-0460/99)
 Subject: Human Rights in Peru
 

Following the 19 July Council demarche on the decision of the Peruvian government and its Congress to withdraw from the jurisdiction of the Inter-American Court of Human Rights, what further action will the Council take?

 
  
 

As a continuation to the Council demarche of 19 July relating to the Peruvian Government and its Congress’ withdrawal from the jurisdiction of the Inter-American Court of Human Rights the Council is following closely the situation of human rights in Peru and all further action by the Peruvian Government in this field.

On 19 July, as the Honourable Member may know, the EU has shown concern for the effects of this decision on the weakening of the regional system of human rights and stressed the fact that Peruvian citizens will be left without the opportunity, provided by Peru’s constitution, of recourse to an international court in case their basic rights are violated.

Among other points, the EU has also recalled paragraph 12 of the Rio Summit Declaration, signed on 29 June 1999 by the Heads of State of the European Union and Latin America and the Caribbean which emphasises the implementation of universal and regional human rights instruments and standards.

Peru’s Deputy acting Foreign Minister recognised before the Troika that this action weakens the regional supervision of human rights. Peru is apparently seeking a review of the Inter-American judicial system and, once that is completed, would consider again acceptance of the Court’s jurisdiction.

The EU position has nonetheless received positive comments in public from the President of the Inter-American Court, Peru’s independent media, the Conference of Bishops, various experts on international law including several ex-Ministers of Foreign Affairs, as well as Peru’s Ombudsman in his comprehensive report. On the other hand, although it is apparent that other countries in the hemisphere do not support Peru’s mode of action, they seem unwilling to enter into direct confrontation with Peru.
